    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="a5f2b159b52e475489cb069c0ca48de7"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a5f2b159b52e475489cb069c0ca48de7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '748937d8-663e-4537-9cf2-ad5ddace7f85'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>School of Materials and Energy, Guangdong University of Technology, Guangzhou, 510006 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Liang/Yaohua">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Lu/Xiaoyi" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaoyi Lu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">
                     <p class="author-name">Xiaoyi Lu</p>
                     <p>School of Materials and Energy, Guangdong University of Technology, Guangzhou, 510006 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Lu/Xiaoyi">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Liu/Jianwen" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Jianwen Liu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Jianwen Liu</p>
                     <p>College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Hubei University, Wuhan, 430062 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Liu/Jianwen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Guo/Zaiping" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Zaiping Guo<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">

                     </ul>
                     <p>School of Chemical Engineering and Advanced Materials, University of Adelaide, Adelaide, South Australia, 5005 Australia</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zpsunxj@gdut.edu.cn"><span>zpsunxj@gdut.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zaiping.guo@adelaide.edu.au"><span>zaiping.guo@adelaide.edu.au</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Guo/Zaiping">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d172626575" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layer-structured bismuth (Bi) is an attractive anode for Na-ion and K-ion batteries due to its large volumetric capacity and suitable redox potentials. However, the cycling stability and rate capability of the Bi anode are restricted by the large volume expansion and sluggish Na/K-storage kinetics. Herein, a structural dimensionality reduction strategy is proposed and developed by converting&nbsp;2D-layer-structured Bi into a quasi-1D structured NiBi<sub>3</sub> with enhanced reaction kinetics and reversibility to realize high-rate and stable cycling performance for Na/K-ion storage. As a proof of concept, the quasi-1D intermetallic NiBi<sub>3</sub> with low formation energy, metallic conductivity, and 3D Na/K-ion diffusion pathways delivers outstanding capacity retention of 94.1% (332 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 15&nbsp;000 cycles for Na-ion storage, and high initial coulombic efficiency of 93.4% with improved capacity retention for K-ion storage. Moreover, investigations on the highly reversible Na/K-storage reaction mechanisms and cycling-driven morphology reconstruction further reveal the origins of the high reversibility and the accommodation to volume expansion. The finding of this work provides a new strategy for high-performance anode design by structural dimensionality manipulation and cycling-driven morphology reconstruction.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202305551-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202305551-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) have recently drawn significant attention as promising alternatives to lithium-ion systems for electric vehicles and large-scale grid storage applications, owing to the abundant Na/K resources and the low standard redox potentials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0001_R_d172626567e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev. Mater. Today">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ion (1.02 Å) and K<sup>+</sup> ion (1.38 Å) than Li<sup>+</sup> ion (0.76 Å) causes limited specific capacity, sluggish intercalation kinetics, and large volume change of the conventional anode material during the cycling, thus hindering their practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0002_R_d172626567e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typically, graphite anode exhibits a smaller capacity and worse cycling performance for Na/K-storage than Li-storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0003_R_d172626567e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, exploring new anode materials with suitable structures for Na/K-ion storage is crucial for developing high-performance Na/K-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0004_R_d172626567e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Bismuth (Bi), with layer structure and semi-metal conductivity, as an alloying anode (385 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) for Na/K-ion batteries has been widely investigated due to its large volumetric capacity (1088 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup> based on Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and 737 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup> based on hexagonal K<sub>3</sub>Bi) and low redox potentials (≈ 0.55&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, ≈ 0.48&nbsp;V vs K<sup>+</sup>/K).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0005_R_d172626567e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the Bi anode undergoes large volume variation during the cycling due to the large ionic radius of Na-ion and K-ion, which causes material pulverization and continuous side reaction between electrode and electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0006_R_d172626567e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the puckered-layer-structure and semi-metal conductivity of the Bi anode restricts the electron transfer and Na/K-ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0005_R_d172626567e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">5</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0007_R_d172626567e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, buffering the massive volume expansion and improving the electron conductivity and Na/K diffusion of Bi anode is crucial for enhancing the electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0008_R_d172626567e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano Small">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To address these issues, constructing nanostructures is an effective method to suppress pulverization due to the void space between nanocomponents that can accommodate the volume expansion induced by Na/K-ion insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0009_R_d172626567e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, previous studies proved that the 3D porous Bi nanoligaments and the Bi nanosheets exhibit improved electrochemical performance than the bulk Bi anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0010_R_d172626567e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the high specific surface area of the nanostructures causes massive side reactions between the electrode and electrolyte, thus reducing the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE). In parallel with nanorefining, carbon modification on Bi anode is another effective way to alleviate volume expansion and simultaneously improve the conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0011_R_d172626567e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Energy Chem. Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Liang and co-workers reported an integrated Bi@C framework superstructure that exhibits excellent cycling stability and ultrahigh rate capability for Na-storage due to its unique structural superiority.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0006_R_d172626567e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Liu and co-workers constructed a spindle-like Bi@N-doped-carbon composite which shows ultrafast and stable K-ion storage owing to the porous structure and fast electron transfer of the carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0008_R_d172626567e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano Small">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the carbon mass-fraction of these Bi@C composites is higher than 20%, corresponding to a mole-fraction of carbon higher than 80%, significantly reducing the volumetric capacity of the electrode.</p>
         
         <p>Beyond nanorefining and carbon modification, combining Bi with other Na/K-active elements (S, Se, Te, et&nbsp;al.) to synthesize the layer-structured Bi-based compound (Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0012_R_d172626567e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Small Methods">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Bi<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0013_R_d172626567e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Bi<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0014_R_d172626567e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and so on) or their heterojunctions is another way to enhance the Na/K-ion diffusion, mitigate the dramatic volume change by the conversion-alloying reaction mechanism, and maintain the volumetric capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0015_R_d172626567e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater. Small">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although these layered Bi-based anodes exhibit 2D Na/K-ion diffusion pathways and large Na/K-storage capacity, these anodes generally induce increased redox potential than Bi due to the high negative electronegativity of their anion components. In addition, most of these Bi-based compounds are semiconductors with high formation energy, which restricts the electronic conductivity and reaction reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0016_R_d172626567e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Chem. Front. Chem. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with these chalcogen elements, the electronegativity of the Na/K-inert transition metal Ni (1.91) is close to Bi (1.9) and leads to low formation energy and weak bond binding of their compounds, which is conducive to the conversion reaction and electrochemical reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0017_R_d172626567e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NiBi<sub>3</sub>, one of the intermetallic compounds of the Ni-Bi system, exhibits a high Bi-content and unique quasi-1D structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0018_R_d172626567e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high Bi-content of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> can maintain the volumetric capacity for Na/K-storage. Moreover, previous reports have shown that the quasi-1D structures are beneficial for promoting ion diffusion in electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0019_R_d172626567e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Energy Storage J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In view of this, we synthesized a quasi-1D structured NiBi<sub>3</sub> intermetallic compound with high Bi-content as anode for Na/K-ion storage by combining layer-structured Bi and Na/K-inactive Ni. Compared with the 2D-layer-structured Bi, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits reduced crystallographic dimensionality and consist of stacked 1D Ni−Bi<sub>3</sub> atomic chains, which form 3D Na/K-ion diffusion pathways. According to the density functional theory (DFT) calculation results, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> shows metallic band structure and low formation energy, which is conducive to the Na/K-storage reaction kinetics and reversibility. Accordingly, when used as anodes for Na/K-storage, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> with a theoretical specific capacity of 351.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> exhibits enhanced cycling stability and high rate-performance. Moreover, the Na/K-ion storage mechanisms and the cycling-driven nanosizing of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode were investigated by ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and ex situ scanning electron microscopy (SEM) to get insight into the superior electrochemical performance origins.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202305551-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202305551-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1a</a>, the puckered-layer-structured Bi and cubic-structured Ni can be directly converted to the quasi-1D structured NiBi<sub>3</sub> by high-energy ball milling. The calculated formation energy of this reaction (−0.00793&nbsp;eV) is negative, which indicates that NiBi<sub>3</sub> is a stable compound and can be synthesized at ambient pressure. In addition, the small value of the formation energy means the Ni–Bi bond is easy to rebuild once they are broken by sodiation/potassiation during the cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0017_R_d172626567e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the XRD pattern evolution of the samples during the NiBi<sub>3</sub> synthesis. Before milling, the diffraction peaks of Ni and Bi precursors can be observed with no other impurity. After milling for 5 h, emerged NiBi<sub>3</sub> diffraction peaks can be detected, which indicates the direct formation of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> phase. Along with the milling time increasing, the peak intensity of Ni and Bi precursors gradually decreased at 10&nbsp;h and disappeared at 15&nbsp;h. Comparatively, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> peaks increased at 10&nbsp;h, and all Ni and Bi precursors transferred into the NiBi<sub>3</sub> phase at 15&nbsp;h. Rietveld XRD refinement was conducted to determine the crystal parameters of the as-synthesized NiBi<sub>3</sub> compound. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1c</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> crystallizes in an orthorhombic Pnma structure, with the lattice parameter <i>a</i> = 8.884 Å, <i>b</i> = 4.100 Å, <i>c</i> = 11.491 Å, <i>α</i> = <i>β</i> = <i>γ</i> = 90°. The crystal structure of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> consists of parallel arranged 1D NiBi<sub>3</sub> chains oriented in the [010] direction, and the channels between the stacked NiBi<sub>3</sub> chains are conducive for the Na/K-ion intercalation and diffusion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1d67724e-825d-46b7-8ae9-be99f483f86f/adma202305551-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1d67724e-825d-46b7-8ae9-be99f483f86f/adma202305551-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c301dbd4-9e9e-4eac-91a8-89f5d44de0aa/adma202305551-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1d67724e-825d-46b7-8ae9-be99f483f86f/adma202305551-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Synthesis of the quasi-1D NiBi<sub>3</sub> from cubic Ni and puckered-layer-structured bismuth. b) XRD pattern evolution of the prepared samples during the NiBi<sub>3</sub> synthesis. c) XRD refinement results of quasi-1D NiBi<sub>3</sub>. d) Full XPS spectrum of NiBi<sub>3</sub>. e,f) High-resolution XPS spectra of Ni 2p (e) and Bi 4f (f). g,h) DFT calculation results of the DOS curves of Bi (g) and NiBi<sub>3</sub> (h). i) Differential charge density of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. The yellow and blue colors indicate positive and negative levels corresponding to the gain and loss of electron density.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterization was conducted on the NiBi<sub>3</sub> compound to evaluate the bonding information between Ni and Bi. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1d</a>, the wide-range spectrum of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> indicates the presence of Ni, Bi, C, and O elements. The high-resolution Ni 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1e</a>) exhibits three 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks which can be indexed to the satellite peak (861.3&nbsp;eV), Ni─O bond (855.6&nbsp;eV), and Ni─Bi bond (852.8&nbsp;eV). The Bi 4f spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1f</a>) exhibits two 4f<sub>7/2</sub> peaks which can be fitted to the Bi─O bond (159.1&nbsp;eV) and NiBi<sub>3</sub> compound (157.0&nbsp;eV). The existence of Ni─O and Bi─O bonds originated the surface oxidization of the sample. The binding energy of the Ni 2p and Bi 4f electrons are very close to the metal Ni (2p<sub>3/2</sub>: 852.6&nbsp;eV) and Bi (4f<sub>7/2</sub>: 157.0&nbsp;eV) due to the weak interaction of the Ni─Bi bonding,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0020_R_d172626567e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is consistent with the small formation energy of NiBi<sub>3</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>To compare the intrinsic electronic conductivity of the Bi and NiBi<sub>3</sub>, their band structure and density of state (DOS) were calculated. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information, the band structure of Bi exhibits a continuous band structure across the fermi level, which indicates that Bi is metallic. However, the corresponding DOS curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1g</a>) shows a slight overlap between the conduction band and valence band around the fermi level, which indicates that the Bi is a semimetal with restricted electron conductivity due to the small electron density at the fermi level. In contrast, the band structure of NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information) shows more electron states are distributed near the fermi level. The corresponding DOS curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1h</a>) exhibits that NiBi<sub>3</sub> is a high electronic conductive metallic compound due to the high electron density at the fermi level, which is contributed by half Ni's and half Bi's electron states. The improved electronic conductivity of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> is beneficial to the Na/K-storage reaction kinetics during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0021_R_d172626567e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Differential charge density and charge density distribution maps were calculated to get insight into the electron interactions between Ni and Bi atoms of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0001">1i</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c,d</a>, Supporting Information, charge accumulation can be observed at the Ni site due to the electrons transfer from Bi to Ni. In addition, continuous electron accumulation also can be observed at 3D channels between each NiBi<sub>3</sub> chain, which is confirmed further by the charge density distribution map of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1e</a>, Supporting Information). The accumulated electrons at the 3D channels are attractive for Na/K-ions and conducive to their diffusion. Moreover, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information, the quasi-1D NiBi<sub>3</sub> shows 3D-diffusion pathways when compared with 2D-diffusion pathways of the layer-structured Bi. Therefore, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> possesses excellent structural superiority for Na/K-storage over Bi.</p>
         
         <p>The SEM images of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2a</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b</a>, Supporting Information) show that the micrometer-sized secondary NiBi<sub>3</sub> particles consist of agglomerated primary nanoparticles. In contrast, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3c,d</a>, Supporting Information, the particle size of the bulk Bi precursor is tens of micrometers, which is larger than that of NiBi<sub>3</sub>. The smaller particle size is benefits Na/K-ion diffusion due to the short diffusion distance. The energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2b</a>) show that the Ni and Bi are homogeneously distributed and matched with the shape of the particle. Further, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) characterization was conducted to observe the grain distribution of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2c</a>, the particle size of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> is consistent with SEM results. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2d,e</a> exhibit the differently oriented nanograins of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> with identified lattice fringe of (302), (112), (101), and (2-11) planes. The corresponding fast Fourier transform (FFT) patterns of the corresponding regions are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2f–h</a>. Particularly, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2g</a> shows the NiBi<sub>3</sub> chains of the (101) plane with the <i>d</i>-space of 7.03 Å, which is well matched with the simulated crystal structure of the right. The electron diffraction pattern of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) exhibits concentric diffraction rings of different NiBi<sub>3</sub> planes, which confirm the polycrystal of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. The EDS mapping results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0002">2i</a>) by the TEM test shows uniformly distributed Ni and Bi elements, and their atomic ratio is ≈ 1:3, confirming the elemental composition of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/745b3974-6f89-487a-b93a-cc1487694930/adma202305551-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/745b3974-6f89-487a-b93a-cc1487694930/adma202305551-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1b2d3a23-fe9d-4143-b7fd-e2054341d2b0/adma202305551-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/745b3974-6f89-487a-b93a-cc1487694930/adma202305551-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM images and b) EDS mapping results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. c) TEM image of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>, d,e) HRTEM images of NiBi<sub>3</sub> lattice with f–h) corresponding FFT patterns. i) EDS mapping results of NiBi<sub>3</sub> by TEM test.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the Na-storage performance of the quasi-1D NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode, the Na//NiBi<sub>3</sub> half-cell was assembled and tested in the electrolyte of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3a</a>, the first discharge of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits a capacity of 387.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The first charge delivers a reversible capacity of 349.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high ICE of 90.3% which is higher than that of the Bi anode (85.3%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the charge–discharge curves of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> show smoother potential transition between different plateaus than Bi anode, which may be due to the improved reaction kinetics and different reaction mechanisms. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NiBi<sub>3</sub>. During the negative scan, a slight current peak at 0.67&nbsp;V was observed, which is different from the apparent peak observed of Bi anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information) at 0.68&nbsp;V of the Bi → NaBi transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0022_R_d172626567e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett. Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, a strong coupled current peak at 0.43&nbsp;V of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode exhibits more significant capacity contribution than the same position peak of the Bi. This mainly corresponds to the different reaction mechanisms for the Na inserting into the Bi and NiBi<sub>3</sub>. During the positive scan, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (0.61 and 0.77&nbsp;V) and Bi (0.62 and 0.76&nbsp;V) anodes present two similar current peaks, which most likely correspond to the similar desodiation process of the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. The cycling performance of NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3c</a>) shows that the capacity remains at 329.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is 94.2% of the first charge capacity (349.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). In contrast, the capacity retention of the Bi anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information) is only 219.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 57.2% of the first charge (383.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), which shows rapid capacity decay than NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode. The rate performance of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6d</a>, Supporting Information) with corresponding charge–discharge curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3d</a>) shows that the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits the reversible capacity of 357.9, 353.8, 350.7, 349.5, 346.0, 339.4, 318.5, and 301.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5, 8, 10, 15, and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Compared with the rate performance of the Bi anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6e,f</a>, Supporting Information), The NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits higher capacity retention than Bi anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3e</a>), and this may due to the improved reaction kinetics of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits a smaller charge transfer resistance (6.5 Ω) than the Bi anode (12.1 Ω).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a94dac4-5f67-41e3-ba8e-7c81b58d16ee/adma202305551-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a94dac4-5f67-41e3-ba8e-7c81b58d16ee/adma202305551-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cda347a2-9e34-4c3d-af2c-d8af1dbded64/adma202305551-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a94dac4-5f67-41e3-ba8e-7c81b58d16ee/adma202305551-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. b) First five CV curves of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode. c) Cycle performance of NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode at the current density of 0.1A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Charge–discharge curves of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> at different current densities. e) Capacity retention comparison of NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi anode. f) ICE comparison of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and previously reported Bi-based anode. g) Cycle performance comparison of NiBi<sub>3</sub>, Bi, and previously reported Bi-based anodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3f</a> shows that the ICE of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode (90.3%) is much higher than previously reported Bi-based anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0006_R_d172626567e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0014_R_d172626567e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0023_R_d172626567e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett. J. Mater. Chem. Energy Storage Mater. Nanoscale ACS Nano Sci. Adv.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This can be attributed to the micrometer particle size, metallic conductivity, and low formation energy of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>, which reduce the side-reaction with electrolyte and improve the reversibility. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0003">3g</a> compares the cycling stability of NiBi<sub>3</sub>, Bi, and the above-mentioned Bi-based anodes. The NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits a reversible capacity of 332.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 15&nbsp;000 cycling at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention of 94.1%, which is much better than the above-mentioned previously reported bismuth-based anodes. In contrast, the bulk Bi anode exhibits a fast capacity decay at 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in a much lower capacity retention of 57.2% and 67.7%, respectively. Also, the Bi anode shows a short circuit failure at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1246 cycles, which may cause by the poor conductivity and large volume expansion induced material crack. To confirm this, the cycled Na//Bi and Na//NiBi<sub>3</sub> coin cell was disassembled to observe the electrode change. As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8 and S9</a>, Supporting Information, the surface morphologies and thickness change of the sodiated and desodiated electrodes of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi at the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle were observed by SEM characterization. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> shows that the electrode surface of the sodiated NiBi<sub>3</sub> is smoother than that of the sodiated Bi, which is beneficial for preventing material detachment and short circuits. Moreover, after following desodiation, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode transformed into a complete nanoporous electrode, and the Bi anode became incomplete, which may be due to the Bi falling off induced by large volume change. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> shows that the thickness of the pristine NiBi<sub>3</sub> (12.4&nbsp;µm) and Bi (12.2&nbsp;µm) electrodes increased to 23.5&nbsp;µm and 36.8&nbsp;µm at the sodiated state of 100th cycle, respectively, which corresponding to the volume expansion of 89% (NiBi<sub>3</sub>) and 202% (Bi). After following desodiation, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> shows a volume change of 19% (based on desodiated state), which is smaller than that of Bi (61%). Further, the short-circuited Na//Bi cell at 1246 cycles were disassembled and observe optically. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information, the Na//Bi cell shows visible material exfoliations, Na dendrite growth, and the active material diffusion from working electrode to the Na metal side after 1246 cycles. In contrast, the Na//NiBi<sub>3</sub> cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrates complete NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode and clean Na metal film, with no dendrite grown after 2000 cycling. The cycling performance of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> at a high rate of 20 C (7 A g<sup>−1</sup>) was investigated, as show in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information, it shows a capacity retention of 93.5% (302.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 930 cycles. Additionally, to investigate the influence of electrolyte choice on this new anode of NiBi<sub>3</sub>, the cycle performance of NiBi<sub>3</sub> was investigated in an ester-based electrolyte (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in PC/FEC). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits a fast capacity decline at initial 20 cycles and a low ICE of 49.1% in PC/FEC electrolyte. This is most likely due to the thick and unstable SEI formation of NiBi<sub>3</sub> in the ester-based electrolyte, which is confirmed by the large charge transfer resistance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information) and consistent with previously reported alloying anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0004_R_d172626567e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0021_R_d172626567e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0024_R_d172626567e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Environ. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To invetigate the Na-storage reaction mechanism of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in diglyme electrolyte, ex situ XRD, in situ EIS, and ex situ SEM analyses were conducted. According to the ex situ XRD results (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0004">4a</a>), the pristine electrode exhibits diffraction peaks of NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Cu only. After discharge to 0.46&nbsp;V, the peak intensity of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> decreased, and new diffraction peaks of the NaBi were detected. With Na continuous insertion, the NaBi converts to the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phase with the Ni detected at 0.43&nbsp;V. And then, all the NaBi phase converts to Na<sub>3</sub>Bi due to the full sodiation at 0.01&nbsp;V. During the charge, the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phase gradually transfers back to NaBi phase with the Na removal at 0.58 and 0.70&nbsp;V, and the NaBi transfer back to the NiBi<sub>3</sub> phase at 1.8&nbsp;V, which indicating the reversibility after sodiation and desodiation reaction. Therefore, a symmetric conversion–alloying reaction mechanism illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0004">4b</a>&nbsp;is proposed and described by the following equations.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202305551-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/db62107c-1a36-482e-aff6-66501b8c1725/adma202305551-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202305551:adma202305551-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adma202305551-math-0001.png">

                           <mrow>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>

                           </mrow>

                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo>⇌</mo>
                           <mrow>

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202305551-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                        <mrow>
                           <mn>3</mn>

                              <mo>+</mo>

                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>

                           </msup>
                           <mo>⇌</mo>
                           <mn>3</mn>

                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>Bi</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} 3\mathrm{NaBi}+6{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}+6{\mathrm{e}}^{-}\rightleftharpoons 3{\mathrm{Na}}_{3}\mathrm{Bi} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6a4afa64-55aa-47ab-b70e-8c14282add39/adma202305551-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6a4afa64-55aa-47ab-b70e-8c14282add39/adma202305551-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/de86d08d-dff1-4f5f-b230-4fee39b0fb8a/adma202305551-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6a4afa64-55aa-47ab-b70e-8c14282add39/adma202305551-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode at the first cycle. b) Electrochemical reaction mechanism of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> with Na during (de)sodiation. c) In situ EIS results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode during the first cycle. d) Morphology evolution and SEM images of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode during cycling. e) SEM image and EDS mapping results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode after 1000 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>According to this reaction mechanism, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode possesses two potential plateaus during the sodiation and desodiation with a theoretical specific capacity of 351.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, consistent with the measured results.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0004">4c</a> presents the in situ EIS spectrum change of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode of the first cycle. Before discharge, the ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) is 4.1 and 12.4 Ω, respectively. After discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> decreased to 3.7 and 4.8 Ω, respectively, which indicates that the generation of the Ni, NaBi, and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phases is beneficial for the charge transfer. After being charged back to 1.8&nbsp;V, the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> decreased to 3.4 Ω further due to the activation of the cell, and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> increased back to 5.1 Ω due to the rebuild of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and disappearance of the Ni, NaBi, and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phases. Notably, after 2000 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c</a>, Supporting Information), the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> are decreased to 3.1 and 2.1 Ω, which is smaller than the initial state. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0004">4d</a> demonstrates the morphology evolution for the spontaneous formation of nanoporous structures of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode during cycling. Before cycling, the macro-sized NiBi<sub>3</sub> particles are embedded in the super P carbon. After 50 cycles, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> transforms to nanosized particles of ≈30&nbsp;nm, and the electrode becomes porous after 500 cycles. After 1000 cycling, it converts to a connected nanosized porous electrode, which is beneficial for alleviating volume expansion during cycling. The EDS mapping of the 1000<sup>th</sup> cycled NiBi<sub>3</sub> particle shows that the Ni and Bi are uniformly distributed, indicating the superior reversibility of the electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0004">4e</a>).</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the kinetic difference between the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi anodes, CV scanning of various scan rates and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were performed. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, the peak currents of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi CV curves are increased with the scan rate during 0.2 to 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. According to the previous study,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0021_R_d172626567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0025_R_d172626567e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the fitted <i>b</i>-value of the log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>) plots of NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information) and Bi (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, supporting information) anode reflect the reaction kinetics of the electrode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0005">5c</a>, the fitted <i>b</i>-value for R<sub>1</sub>, R<sub>2</sub>, O<sub>2</sub>, and O<sub>1</sub> peaks of Bi is 0.519, 0.515, 0.522, and 0.493, respectively, which is close to 0.5. This indicates a diffusion-controlled reaction process which corresponds to the slow Na diffusion kinetics restricted by the reasons of large particle size, low electronic conductivity, and puckered-2D Na-ion diffusion pathways of the bulk Bi particles. In contrast, the fitted <i>b</i>-value for R<sub>1</sub>, R<sub>2</sub>, O<sub>2</sub>, and O<sub>1</sub> of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> peaks is 0.799, 0.701, 0.577, and 0.570, respectively, which is higher than that of the Bi anode and close to 1 for R<sub>1</sub> and R<sub>2</sub>. This indicates that NiBi<sub>3</sub> possesses fast reaction kinetics than Bi, which is because of the smaller particle size, metallic conductivity, and 3D Na-ion diffusion pathways of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>. To confirm this, the Na-ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) of NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi anode were investigated by GITT tests. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0005">5d,e</a>, The NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode shows smaller potential polarization than Bi at the initial Na-insertion process, indicating that the Na-ion is easier to insert into NiBi<sub>3</sub> than Bi. The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0005">5f</a>. During sodiation, especially at the initial Na-ion insertion, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> shows much higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> than Bi, which indicates that the 3D diffusion pathways of the quasi-1D NiBi<sub>3</sub> are more conducive to Na-insertion. During desodiation, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi exhibit closed D<sub>Na</sub> values, which is due to the similar desodiation reaction of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and NaBi.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fad2207f-2edb-4629-8d88-6e15dc266112/adma202305551-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fad2207f-2edb-4629-8d88-6e15dc266112/adma202305551-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5fdde96c-40e5-42f3-b3c1-e5c9b21202a2/adma202305551-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fad2207f-2edb-4629-8d88-6e15dc266112/adma202305551-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves at various scan rates of a) NiBi<sub>3</sub> and b) Bi. c) Corresponding fitted <i>b</i>-value of the redox peaks of NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi. d,e) GITT test results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (d) and Bi (e) anode. f) Calculated Na-ion diffusion coefficient of NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi from GITT results.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the practical feasibility of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in full cell system, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) was used as the cathode material. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a–c</a>, Supporting Information, shows the working principle of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells. The average potential of the cathode and anode were 3.37 and 0.53&nbsp;V, respectively, corresponding to a high voltage of 2.84&nbsp;V for the full cell. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d</a>, Supporting Information, shows the initial nine cycles charge–discharge curves of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, the initial charge–discharge shows the capacities of 99.3/103.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a high ICE of 96.1%, and the average output voltage at the 9th cycle is 2.84&nbsp;V, which is consisted with the calculated result. The rate-performance of the full cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16e</a>, Supporting Information) shows the capacities of 99.3, 86.9, 81.3, 78.4, 75.7, 72.5, and 62.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.5, 1, 1.5, 2, 2.5, 3, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The cycle performance of the NiBi<sub>3</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16f</a>, Supporting Information) shows a capacity retention of 80.8% after 150 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>Given the excellent Na-storage performance of NiBi<sub>3</sub> and the similar properties of K to Na, the K-storage performance of the as-prepared NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode was evaluated in the electrolyte of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0006">6a</a>, the first potassiation of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode shows a capacity of 349.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The depotassiation process exhibits a reversible capacity of 326.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the ICE of 93.4%, which is higher than that of the Bi anode (91.5%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a>, Supporting Information). The dQ/dV plots of NiBi<sub>3</sub> of the first tree cycles are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0006">6b</a>. During the potassiation, a slight current peak at 0.89&nbsp;V and a main coupled current peak at 0.37&nbsp;V were detected, which is different from the three obvious three peaks at 0.91, 0.45, and 0.37&nbsp;V of bulk Bi anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a>, Supporting Information). The main capacity contribution of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> is from the peak at 0.37&nbsp;V. In contrast, three peaks of the Bi anode have significant contributions to the capacity. This could be due to the different reaction mechanisms of K insert into NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode Bi. During the depotassiation, the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits three current peaks at 0.97, 0.80, and 1.20&nbsp;V, which is similar to the Bi anode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0006">6c</a>, The NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits stable cycling performance with a reversible capacity of 323.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycling at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 92.5%. In contrast, the Bi anode shows a fast capacity decay at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in a much lower capacity retention of 19.6% after 50 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17c</a>, Supporting Information). The rate-performance of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information) exhibits reversible capacities of 337.6, 326.7, 312.7, 300.7, and 286.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 0.5, 1, 2, 3, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb6386cb-5096-4010-90eb-e66e2a54ea07/adma202305551-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb6386cb-5096-4010-90eb-e66e2a54ea07/adma202305551-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b6254f29-587a-4f28-82f8-f3e84d88aaed/adma202305551-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb6386cb-5096-4010-90eb-e66e2a54ea07/adma202305551-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of NiBi<sub>3</sub>. b) First three dQ/dV curves of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode. c) Cycle performance of NiBi<sub>3</sub> at current density of 0.5A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Normalized first cycle charge–discharge curves with ICE results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in the different electrolytes (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KFSI in EC/DEC, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in DME, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC/FEC). e) EIS spectra of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in different electrolytes. f) Fitted <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value and calculated diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>K</sub>) of NiBi<sub>3</sub> in different electrolytes. g) Cycling performance of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in different electrolytes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As we know, electrolytes have a great influence on the alloying anode for K-storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0006_R_d172626567e1662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202305551-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0026_R_d172626567e1662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the K-storage performance of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode is tested in another three electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0006">6d</a> compares the first cycle of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode for K-storage in different electrolytes. The NiBi<sub>3</sub> shows the highest ICE of 93.4% in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme electrolyte, which is higher than that of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KFSI in EC/DEC (68.3%), 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in DME (61.7%), and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC/FEC (41.6%). In addition, the proportion of the consumed K-ions during the SEI formation process in different electrolytes is 7.2% (diglyme), 10.3% (EC/DEC), 20.3% (DME), and 49.6% (EC/DEC/FEC) of their first discharge capacity, respectively. Therefore, the high ICE of NiBi<sub>3</sub> in the diglyme electrolyte originated from its milder SEI formation reaction compared to the other three electrolytes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0006">6e,f</a> compares the EIS results and fitted <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in these four different electrolytes. The NiBi<sub>3</sub> shows an <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 13.1 Ω in diglyme electrolyte, which is smaller than that in DME (1243 Ω), EC/DEC (2715 Ω), and EC/DEC/FEC (6680 Ω) electrolytes. Moreover, the calculated K-ion diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>K</sub>) by EIS results in these electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information) show that the NiBi<sub>3</sub> exhibits the highest <i>D</i><sub>K</sub> of 7.16 × 10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in diglyme electrolyte, which is higher than others. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0006">6g</a> compares the cycling stability of NiBi<sub>3</sub> in different electrolytes. Its exhibits a reversible capacity of 287.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycling at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> in diglyme electrolyte with a capacity retention of 93.0%. In contrast, fast capacity decay in EC/DEC, DME, and EC/DEC/FEC electrolytes were observed, with the low capacity retention of 47.3%, 44.0%, and 4.9%, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>To get insight into the K-storage reaction mechanism of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode in diglyme electrolyte, ex situ XRD, in situ EIS, and ex situ SEM investigations were performed. The ex situ XRD results are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202305551-fig-0007">7a</a>. Before discharge, the diffraction peaks of the unreacted NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Cu current collector can be observed. When discharged to 0.29&nbsp;V, the peak intensity of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> decreased, with no other new peak detected. With continuous Na insertion, new diffraction peaks of the cubic K<sub>3</sub>Bi, hexagonal K<sub>3</sub>Bi, and Ni metal were detected at 0.27 and 0.01&nbsp;V, indicating the direct conversion of NiBi<sub>3</sub> into Ni and K<sub>3</sub>Bi by K-ion insertion. Notably, previously reported KBi<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>3</sub>Bi<sub>2</sub> intermediate phases of Bi anode during potassiation were not detected for the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode, which indicates the quasi-1D structure of NiBi<sub>3</sub> accelerates the potassiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202305551-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202305551-bib-0026_R_d172626567e1774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is consistent with the difference in the discharge dQ/dV peaks of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> and Bi. During the charge, the cubic and hexagonal K<sub>3</sub>Bi gradually convert to the KBi<sub>2</sub> phase along with the potential increases and continuous Na removal at 0.67 and 1.06&nbsp;V. Finally, the KBi<sub>2</sub> and Ni recombine into NiBi<sub>3</sub> at 1.8&nbsp;V, which indicates the reversibility of NiBi<sub>3</sub> after potassiation and despotassiation reactions. Therefore, an asymmetric reaction mechanism illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202305551-fig-0007">7b</a>&nbsp;is proposed and described by the following equations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202305551-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7d26e484-6245-44d1-8e1d-d05dd5c85dca/adma202305551-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7d26e484-6245-44d1-8e1d-d05dd5c85dca/adma202305551-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/81c581aa-37b4-4010-a9d3-1fd41250bf6c/adma202305551-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7d26e484-6245-44d1-8e1d-d05dd5c85dca/adma202305551-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202305551-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202305551" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode at the first cycle for K-storage. b) Electrochemical reaction mechanism of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> with K. c) In situ EIS results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> anode during the first cycle. d) Morphology evolution and SEM images of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode during cycling. e) SEM image and EDS mapping results of the NiBi<sub>3</sub> electrode after 50 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Potassiation

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202305551-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/857b2515-41c9-4a3e-a1d1-db7558172c7b/adma202305551-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202305551:adma202305551-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adma202305551-math-0003.png">

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{NiBi}}_{\mathrm{3}}{\mathrm{ + 9 }}{{\mathrm{K}}}^{\mathrm{ + }}{\mathrm{ + 9 }}{{\mathrm{e}}}^{\mathrm{ - }} \to {\mathrm{Ni + 3 }}{{\mathrm{K}}}_{\mathrm{3}}{\mathrm{Bi}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Depotassiation

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202305551-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
